Procedure and Conditions for Annulment Due to Husband's Insanity
Procedure and conditions for wife's annulment due to husband's insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four conditions for a wife to annul marriage due to husband's insanity and apply the procedure to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Annulment for insanity is a hila with strict conditions: qadi decree, one-year wait, no consent, and council-limited option.

Annulment (faskh) due to a husband's insanity is a serious step that must not be taken lightly. This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah provides a precise procedure and four essential conditions that a wife must meet to obtain annulment. Understanding these conditions prevents misuse and ensures the annulment is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the act
Recognize that this is a hila (relief measure) for a wife trapped in a marriage with a dangerously insane husband. It is not a divorce (talaq) but an annulment (faskh) under strict judicial supervision.
2
Trace the procedure step by step
The wife must apply to the qadi, prove dangerous insanity, receive a one-year treatment respite, then reapply. If insanity persists, the qadi gives the option, which she must exercise in the same council.
3
Extract the four conditions
From the passage, list: (a) no prior consent, (b) no voluntary intercourse after learning, (c) decree by qadi or authority, (d) option exercised in the same council.
These conditions are cumulative.
4
Understand the faskh/talaq distinction
Note that if insanity existed at marriage, the annulment is faskh not talaq. If insanity occurred after marriage, it is ambiguous; Maliki scholars may be consulted.
This distinction will be explored in the next lesson.
5
Apply ethical caution
The author stresses 'severe necessity' and 'complete honesty'. The mufti must guard against even perceived manipulation of the rules.

باطل ہو گیا۔
رو به جنون کی عزت و شہر کا شرعی حکم مراد رات کا حل یہ ہے کہ گرفت صحیحہ سے قبل نکاح فسخ ہو گیا ہے
علام تبدیل مجلس کا بیان چنین کے بیان میں گزر چکا ہے اس کو دیکھ لیا جائے ١٣ منہ
Without severe necessity the marriage should not be annulled.
(2) The method of annulment separation: The way for the wife of an insane man to annul the marriage is to apply in the court of the qadi. And she must prove that the husband is dangerously insane. The qadi investigates the matter; if the case is proven true, he gives a one-year respite for treatment to the insane man. If after the year passes, she applies again and the husband's insanity still exists, then the woman is given the option. Then if the woman demands annulment in the same council in which she was given the option, the qadi effects the separation. And if this separation is due to insanity that existed at the time of marriage, it is not divorce but annulment (faskh). If the separation is due to insanity that occurred after marriage, then there is probability that it is divorce, so scholars of the Maliki school should be consulted. Until investigation, it should be considered divorce as a precaution. Conditions for annulment of the wife of an insane man: For the wife of an insane man to obtain the option of annulment, the following conditions must exist. If these conditions are not found, she has no right to separation; therefore it is necessary to understand them carefully.
(a) First condition: There should be no consent from the wife. If before marriage she knew of the insanity and still married, she does not get the option of annulment. If the insanity occurred after marriage, the condition is that after learning of the insanity she did not express consent to remain in the marriage. If she expressed consent even once, the option of annulment becomes void.
(b) Second condition: After learning of the insanity, the woman should not have willingly given opportunity for intercourse or [؟dava'i jarij?]. However, if the insane man forced intercourse etc., then the option is not invalidated.
(Benefit): If expression of consent or giving opportunity for intercourse etc. occurred after the insanity that gives the option, then the option will not remain. But if she consented to remain in minor insanity or gave opportunity for intercourse etc., and later the insanity increased, then from that consent and permission the option of annulment is not invalidated. If one benefits from this leniency, one must act with complete honesty and extreme caution.
(c) Third condition: Just like the proper wife, the wife of an insane man is not independent in separating from her husband; rather, the decree of the qadi is a condition. And if in a locality there is no qadi, then the Muslim ruler should be appealed to, provided he has been given authority from the government to settle such matters and decides in the Shari‘a way. Otherwise, application should be made to the Muslim community, whose conditions have passed in the previous case; they must be seen.
(d) Fourth condition: When after the one-year respite has passed, upon renewed application the qadi gives the option, the woman's right to separation remains only for that council. If the council is dissolved, or the woman leaves by herself or due to someone else's prompting, or the council changes in any way, then the option of annulment becomes void.
(Regarding the ruling of madness in terms of honor and reputation: the solution is that the contract was annulled before valid [؟grip?]. The explanation of change of council has passed in the explanation of such; it should be seen. 13 minh?
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah details the procedure and conditions for a wife to annul her marriage due to her husband's insanity. It begins by emphasizing that annulment should not be undertaken without severe necessity. The proper method is to apply to a qadi, prove the husband's dangerous insanity, and then the qadi grants a one-year waiting period for treatment. If after that year the insanity persists, the qadi gives the woman the option to demand annulment in that same gathering. A key distinction is made: if the insanity existed at the time of marriage, the annulment is a faskh (voiding) not a talaq (divorce). If the insanity occurred after marriage, the matter is ambiguous and Maliki scholars should be consulted; as a precaution, it should be treated as a talaq. The author then lists four essential conditions for the wife to retain the option of annulment: (a) she must not have consented to the marriage knowing the insanity, or if the insanity arose later, she must not have expressed acceptance of the situation; (b) she must not have willingly allowed intercourse or similar after learning of the insanity, though forced intercourse does not invalidate the option; a benefit note clarifies that if she accepted minor insanity which later worsened, the option remains; (c) the annulment must be decreed by a qadi or a competent Muslim authority, not done unilaterally by the wife; (d) the option is limited to the council in which the qadi grants it—if the council changes or the woman leaves, the option lapses. This strict procedure ensures that annulment is not abused.

Key Terms
فسخ نکاح (faskh al-nikah): Annulment of marriage due to a legal defect, as opposed to divorce (talaq).
خیار فسخ (khiyar al-faskh): The option to annul the marriage granted to the wife upon specific conditions.
جنون (junun): Insanity or madness, a legal defect that gives grounds for annulment.
مجنون (majnun): An insane person, specifically the husband in this context.
مہلت (muhlat): The one-year respite given by the qadi for treatment of the insane husband.
مجلس (majlis): The council or sitting of the qadi where the option is granted and must be exercised.
رضامندی (razamandi): Consent or acceptance by the wife to remain in the marriage after knowledge of insanity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the conditions for a wife to obtain annulment due to husband's insanity?
A) She must not have consented after knowledge of insanity.
B) She must not have willingly allowed intercourse after knowledge.
C) She must obtain a decree from a qadi or competent Muslim authority.
D) She must exercise the option within one year of the qadi's decree.

Key Takeaway
Annulment due to husband's insanity in Hanafi fiqh requires strict judicial procedure and four cumulative conditions; without them, the annulment is invalid.

Faskh vs Talaq: Classification of Annulment for Supervening Insanity
Faskh vs talaq distinction in annulment due to husband's insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi distinction between faskh and talaq in cases of husband's insanity and identify the precautionary ruling.

Core Idea
When insanity arises after marriage, annulment is ambiguous; Hanafi precaution treats it as talaq to avoid counting errors.

The previous lesson outlined the procedure and conditions for annulment. This lesson delves into the crucial distinction in legal classification: whether the annulment is considered faskh (voiding) or talaq (divorce). The passage shows that if insanity existed at marriage, it is faskh; if after marriage, it is ambiguous and requires consultation with Maliki scholars. As a precaution, it is treated as talaq.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two scenarios
Identify: insanity existing at time of marriage vs. insanity occurring after marriage.
The passage explicitly distinguishes these.
2
Identify the ruling for each
For pre-existing insanity: faskh (annulment). For supervening insanity: ambiguous, with precaution of talaq.
Faskh does not count as a divorce; talaq does.
3
Understand thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: faskh does not affect the count of divorces, while talaq does. Also revocability may differ.
This is why Maliki consultation is sought.
4
Analyze the precautionary ruling
Hanafi scholars opt to treat it as talaq until clarification from Maliki sources, to avoid undercounting divorces.
This is ihtiyat (precaution).
5
Note the role of Maliki consultation
The passage advises consulting Maliki scholars to resolve the ambiguity.
This shows intra-madhhab cooperation.

علام تبدیل مجلس کا بیان چنین کے بیان میں گزر چکا ہے اس کو دیکھ لیا جائے ١٣ منہ
Without severe necessity the marriage should not be annulled.
(2) The method of annulment separation: The way for the wife of an insane man to annul the marriage is to apply in the court of the qadi. And she must prove that the husband is dangerously insane. The qadi investigates the matter; if the case is proven true, he gives a one-year respite for treatment to the insane man. If after the year passes, she applies again and the husband's insanity still exists, then the woman is given the option. Then if the woman demands annulment in the same council in which she was given the option, the qadi effects the separation. And if this separation is due to insanity that existed at the time of marriage, it is not divorce but annulment (faskh). If the separation is due to insanity that occurred after marriage, then there is probability that it is divorce, so scholars of the Maliki school should be consulted. Until investigation, it should be considered divorce as a precaution. Conditions for annulment of the wife of an insane man: For the wife of an insane man to obtain the option of annulment, the following conditions must exist. If these conditions are not found, she has no right to separation; therefore it is necessary to understand them carefully.
(a) First condition: There should be no consent from the wife. If before marriage she knew of the insanity and still married, she does not get the option of annulment. If the insanity occurred after marriage, the condition is that after learning of the insanity she did not express consent to remain in the marriage. If she expressed consent even once, the option of annulment becomes void.
(b) Second condition: After learning of the insanity, the woman should not have willingly given opportunity for intercourse or [؟dava'i jarij?]. However, if the insane man forced intercourse etc., then the option is not invalidated.
(Benefit): If expression of consent or giving opportunity for intercourse etc. occurred after the insanity that gives the option, then the option will not remain. But if she consented to remain in minor insanity or gave opportunity for intercourse etc., and later the insanity increased, then from that consent and permission the option of annulment is not invalidated. If one benefits from this leniency, one must act with complete honesty and extreme caution.
(c) Third condition: Just like the proper wife, the wife of an insane man is not independent in separating from her husband; rather, the decree of the qadi is a condition. And if in a locality there is no qadi, then the Muslim ruler should be appealed to, provided he has been given authority from the government to settle such matters and decides in the Shari‘a way. Otherwise, application should be made to the Muslim community, whose conditions have passed in the previous case; they must be seen.
(d) Fourth condition: When after the one-year respite has passed, upon renewed application the qadi gives the option, the woman's right to separation remains only for that council. If the council is dissolved, or the woman leaves by herself or due to someone else's prompting, or the council changes in any way, then the option of annulment becomes void.
(Regarding the ruling of madness in terms of honor and reputation: the solution is that the contract was annulled before valid [؟grip?]. The explanation of change of council has passed in the explanation of such; it should be seen. 13 minh?
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah details the procedure and conditions for a wife to annul her marriage due to her husband's insanity. It begins by emphasizing that annulment should not be undertaken without severe necessity. The proper method is to apply to a qadi, prove the husband's dangerous insanity, and then the qadi grants a one-year waiting period for treatment. If after that year the insanity persists, the qadi gives the woman the option to demand annulment in that same gathering. A key distinction is made: if the insanity existed at the time of marriage, the annulment is a faskh (voiding) not a talaq (divorce). If the insanity occurred after marriage, the matter is ambiguous and Maliki scholars should be consulted; as a precaution, it should be treated as a talaq. The author then lists four essential conditions for the wife to retain the option of annulment: (a) she must not have consented to the marriage knowing the insanity, or if the insanity arose later, she must not have expressed acceptance of the situation; (b) she must not have willingly allowed intercourse or similar after learning of the insanity, though forced intercourse does not invalidate the option; a benefit note clarifies that if she accepted minor insanity which later worsened, the option remains; (c) the annulment must be decreed by a qadi or a competent Muslim authority, not done unilaterally by the wife; (d) the option is limited to the council in which the qadi grants it—if the council changes or the woman leaves, the option lapses. This strict procedure ensures that annulment is not abused.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment that voids the marriage contract from its inception (or from the defect) without counting as divorce.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement or by court order in certain cases.
احتیاط (ihtiyat): Precaution; adopting the more stringent ruling in cases of doubt.
احتمال (ihtimal): Probability; the passage says there is probability that it is talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should the Hanafi mufti do when presented with a case of annulment due to post-marriage insanity?
A) Rule it as faskh without any investigation.
B) Rule it as talaq definitively.
C) Consult Maliki scholars and treat it as talaq until investigation.
D) Refer the case entirely to Maliki scholars.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, annulment for post-marriage insanity is treated as talaq as a precaution until Maliki consultation clarifies the classification.

Hila for Annulment Due to Lack of Maintenance Using Maliki School
Hila for wife to annul marriage when husband is insane and cannot provide maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa adopting another madhhab's ruling as a hila when Hanafi law provides no relief, identifying the conditions and procedural requirements.

Core Idea
A Hanafi mufti may adopt the Maliki ruling for faskh due to non-maintenance only after verifying all Maliki conditions and consulting peers.

The wife of an insane husband may find herself trapped: Hanafi law does not permit annulment (faskh) solely based on non-provision of maintenance, and the specific conditions for faskh due to insanity may be absent. In such distressed circumstances, Ashraf Ali Thanwi outlines a legal stratagem (hila): the mufti, after thorough investigation and consultation, can issue a fatwa based on the Maliki school, which allows a wife to seek annulment when the husband fails to provide nafaqa. This lesson dissects the conditions and cautions required for this exceptional recourse, emphasizing that it is not a casual option but a carefully constrained tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi impasse
Understand that under Hanafi law, non-provision of maintenance alone does not give the wife a right to faskh; nor does insanity automatically annul the marriage unless specific conditions (like incurable insanity causing harm) are met. If those conditions are absent, the wife has no Hanafi remedy.
The passage assumes the Hanafi conditions for faskh due to insanity are not present.
2
Recognize the need for takhayyur (selection of another school)
When the wife is in extreme distress (idtirar) and no Hanafi relief is available, the mufti may look to another madhhab. In this case, the Maliki school permits faskh for non-maintenance, offering an exit.
Takhayyur is permissible only if it does not lead to a more harmful outcome and all conditions of the chosen school are met.
3
Verify the key Maliki condition
The Maliki school conditions faskh on the wife's lack of knowledge of the husband's poverty at the time of marriage. If she knew, she cannot seek annulment. This condition prevents unfairness.
The passage lists only this condition explicitly; other conditions must be obtained from Maliki sources.
4
Determine the legal effect of the separation
The annulment under Maliki law is treated as a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) within the Hanafi framework. This means the husband can take her back during iddah, and maintenance is due during that period.
This ruling protects the wife's rights during iddah and avoids creating a situation worse than the original.
5
Adhere to process: consultation and caution
The mufti must thoroughly investigate the woman's distress, consult with several scholars, and ensure all conditions of the Maliki school are fulfilled. The hila is not to be applied casually.
The passage stresses 'extreme care' and adherence to all Maliki conditions, which are detailed in Maliki fiqh works.

(Benefit) If the maintenance of the wife of an insane person is not arranged, and the conditions mentioned above for annulment of marriage due to insanity of the husband are not present in a place, then separation cannot occur due to insanity. But if the insane husband has no source of income and he has the ability to earn, and there is no arrangement for the wife's food and maintenance, then in such a case, after thorough investigation of the woman's distress and consultation with several scholars, there is scope for the mufti to give a fatwa that according to the Maliki school, due to lack of maintenance of food and expenses, the qadhi or his representative (sharia committee or Muslim ruler) may effect separation between them, and this separation will be in the nature of a revocable divorce. But this requires extreme care and adherence to all conditions of the Maliki school, among which one condition is that annulment due to lack of maintenance can only occur if the wife did not know of the husband's poverty before marriage; otherwise, if the marriage was contracted with knowledge of his poverty, she has no right to demand separation due to lack of maintenance. The remaining conditions can be known from Maliki books when needed. Summary of rulings on faskh of marriage for the wife of a missing person: general and even special people hearing this...

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) for a difficult situation: when a wife of an insane husband cannot obtain annulment under Hanafi law because the specific conditions for faskh due to insanity are not met, but there is also a lack of maintenance. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, permits the mufti, after careful investigation and consultation, to issue a fatwa based on the Maliki school. In Maliki law, a wife can seek annulment (faskh) if the husband cannot provide nafaqa (maintenance), even if he is not insane. This is a form of takhayyur (selection of another school) for necessity. The separation is treated as a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), meaning the husband can take her back during iddah. However, the author emphasizes strict conditions: the wife must not have known of the husband's poverty at marriage; if she did, the option is lost. Other Maliki conditions must also be verified from Maliki sources. This reflects the Hanafi practice of recourse to other schools in exceptional circumstances, with utmost caution and adherence to all conditions of that school.

Key Terms
فتویٰ (fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a mufti, here based on Maliki school for a specific case.
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce during the iddah period, allowing the husband to take back his wife without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti may, in cases of extreme necessity and after strict verification, adopt the Maliki ruling for annulment due to lack of maintenance, but he must adhere to all conditions of the Maliki school and act with utmost caution.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems

Stratagem for Annulment When Husband Insane and No Maintenance
Provides a legal stratagem to dissolve marriage when husband is insane and cannot provide maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conditions under which a mufti may use the Maliki school to dissolve a marriage when the husband is insane and cannot provide maintenance.

Core Idea
When Hanafi conditions for dissolution fail, the Maliki school offers a stratagem under strict necessity.

This lesson examines a critical legal stratagem (hila) from the Hanafi tradition, addressing a dire situation: a woman married to an insane husband who cannot provide maintenance. While standard Hanafi law limits grounds for dissolution, the text introduces a permissible method to adopt the Maliki position under strict conditions. By the end, you will understand not only the procedure but also the ethical and epistemological boundaries of such cross-madhhab fatwa-giving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem and Default Rule
Recognize that the husband is insane (junun) and unable to provide maintenance (nafaqah). The default Hanafi rule: no dissolution if the specific conditions for annulment due to insanity are absent.
The text refers to 'conditions previously mentioned' — they are not reproduced here but assumed known.
2
Establish Necessity (Darura)
The mufti must thoroughly investigate the woman's distress (idtirar) and consult other scholars to confirm that the situation is dire and no other remedy exists.
Necessity is the core trigger for adopting the Maliki school.
3
Apply the Maliki School with Conditions
The dissolution (faskh) is effected by a qadi or shari'ah committee, and it counts as a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). But all Maliki conditions must be met — especially that the wife did not know of the husband's poverty at the time of marriage.
Other conditions are found in Maliki books.
4
Note the Limits and Precautions
The mufti must act with extreme caution (ghaur wa fikr), consult scholars, and adhere strictly to the Maliki school's conditions. The text warns that if the wife knew of poverty beforehand, the option to seek dissolution is forfeited.
This prevents misuse of the stratagem.

Source Text

فائدہ اگر زوجہ مجنون کے نفقہ کا انتظام نہ ہو مجنون کی ہوی کا نکاح فسخ ہونے کے لیے جو شرائط اورپر مذکور ہیں اگر کسی جگہ وہ شرائط موجود نہ ہوں تو جنون کی وجہ سے تفریق نہیں ہوسکتی لیکن اگر مجنون کی کس قسم کی آمدنی کا ذریعہ نہ ہواوریہ اس کو روز گار حاصل کرنے پر قدرت ہو اور بیوی کے لیے نان و نفقہ کا کوئی انتظام نہ ہو تو ایسی و صورت میں مفتی کے لیے عورت کے اضطرار کی پوری تحقیق ہو جائے اور چند علماء سے مشورہ کے بعد اسی فتوسی کی بھی گنجائش ہے کہ مذہب مالکیہ کی وجہ سے نان و نفقہ کے انتظام نہ ہونے کی صورت میں قاضی یا اس کا قائم مقام شرعی کمیٹی یا مسلم حاکم) ان دونوں میں تفریق کر دے اور یہ تفریق طلاق رجعی کے حکم میں ہوگی، لیکن اس میں نہایت غور و فکر سے کام لے کر مذہب مالکیہ کی تمام شرائط کی پابندی ضروری ہے جن میں سے ایک شرط یہ بھی ہے کہ نان و نفقہ کا انتظام نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے فسخ نکاح اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جب کہ نکاح سے پہلے اس کو شوہر کے مفلس و نادار ہونے کا علم نہ ہو ورنہ اگرناداری کا علم ہوتے ہوئے نکاح کیا گیا ہے تو اب نان ونفقہ کے انتظام نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے بھی اس کو تفریق کے مطالبہ کاحتی نہ ہوگا اور باقی شرائگا۔ نہ اسی مسئلہ کی بوقت ضرورت کتب مالکیہ سے معلوم ہو سکتی ہیں جن کے نام گذر چکے۔
Benefit: If the provision of maintenance for the wife of an insane person is not arranged, and the conditions previously mentioned for annulment of the marriage of the insane person are not present at any place, then separation cannot occur due to insanity. But if the insane person has no source of income and is unable to earn a livelihood, and there is no arrangement for the wife's food and maintenance, then in such a situation, after fully investigating the woman's distress and consulting several scholars, the mufti may also give a fatwa that, on the basis of the Maliki school, the qadi or his deputy (a shari'ah committee or Muslim ruler) may separate them. This separation will be in the ruling of a revocable divorce. However, this must be done with extreme deliberation, and it is necessary to adhere to all conditions of the Maliki school. Among these, one condition is that annulment of marriage due to lack of maintenance is only possible when she did not know before marriage that the husband was insolvent and poor. Otherwise, if she married knowing his poverty, then she does not have the right to demand separation due to lack of maintenance. The remaining conditions can be learned from Maliki books when needed, the names of which have been mentioned.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) for a woman married to an insane husband who cannot provide maintenance. In Hanafi fiqh, if the specific conditions for annulment due to insanity are not met, the marriage cannot be dissolved. However, in a state of necessity, the mufti may consider the Maliki school, which allows dissolution due to the husband's inability to provide maintenance. The mufti must first confirm the woman's distress and consult with other scholars. The dissolution is effected by a qadi or a Shari'ah committee and is considered a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i). Crucially, all Maliki conditions must be observed, such as the wife not knowing of the husband's poverty at the time of marriage. If she knew, the option is forfeited. Other conditions are detailed in Maliki fiqh books. This demonstrates the concept of talfiq (combining schools) under necessity, but with strict adherence to the conditions of the borrowed school.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter, that a husband is obliged to provide to his wife.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or dissolution of the marriage contract by the qadi or by operation of law, not by talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The couple must have children.

Key Takeaway
In extreme necessity, a Hanafi mufti may adopt the Maliki ruling on dissolution for non-maintenance, but only after verifying all Maliki conditions, especially the wife's ignorance of the husband's poverty at marriage.

Second Verdict for Missing Wife: Recommended, Not Obligatory
Ruling on the need for a second judge's verdict after waiting period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the wife of a missing person to determine whether a second judge's verdict is necessary, considering hardship.

Core Idea
A second verdict for the missing wife is recommended, not obligatory, if reapplication is difficult.

This lesson addresses a practical hila (legal stratagem) for the wife of a missing person (zawjah mafqud) who has waited four years per a judge's order. The Hanafi school generally recommends obtaining a second verdict confirming death for caution, but the Maliki school does not require it. Ashraf Ali Thanwi clarifies that when reapplication is burdensome, the wife may act without the second verdict, following the easier Maliki view as a concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and Hanafi default
State the problem: the wife of a missing person has waited four years after a judge's investigation. The Hanafi default is that she should apply again for a death ruling for precaution.
2
Contrast with the Maliki view
The Maliki school holds that the initial verdict suffices; no second application is needed. Identify this as the opposing school's position.
3
Understand the Hanafi accommodation
Thanwi notes that the Hanafi school allows omitting the second verdict when reapplication is very difficult (mashaqqah), thereby granting a concession that aligns with the Maliki position.
This is a talfiq or takhayyur-like accommodation.
4
Apply the hardship exception
Determine whether reapplication is genuinely difficult (e.g., distance, lack of access to judge). If so, the wife may remarry without a second verdict.
The exception is only for genuine hardship.
5
Formulate the fatwa
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the second verdict is mustahabb (recommended) but not wajib; in hardship, it can be omitted. State this clearly for a fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زوجہ مفقود کے لیے چار سال انتظار کے بعد دوبارہ درخواست دینا اور اب چار سال گزرنے کے بعد دوبارہ قاضی کی عدالت میں درخواست دینا اور عدت وفات کے لیے فیصلہ حاصل کرنا، مالکیہ کے نہ دیک ضروری نہیں بلکہ قاضی کا فیصلہ صرف پہلی مرتبہ مات دینے کے وقت ضروری ہے دینی جس وقت قاضی نے عورت کا دعوی سن کر تحقیق کر کے چار سال کی ،اذیت ، انتظار کے لیے دے دی صرف اُسی وقت قاضی کا فیصلہ ضروری ہے، لیکن احتیاط اس میں ہے کہ جب وہ چار سال جو قاضی نے مقرر کئے تھے پورے ہو جائیں تو دوبارہ درخواست دے کر قاضی سے مفقود کی موت کا حکم حاصل کر لیا جائے تا کہ منفی مذہب میں گنجائش کی حد تک رعایت ہو جائے لیکن جس جگہ قاضی وغیرہ کی طرف دوسری مرتبه در خواست دینا مقدمہ پیش کرنا زیادہ دشوار ہو وہاں پر بغیر دوسری مرتبہ متقدمہ پیش کیئے ہوئے عمل کر لینے میں حرج نہیں ہے۔
For the wife of a missing person, after four years of waiting, reapplying and now after four years have passed, reapplying to the judge's court and obtaining a verdict for the waiting period of death (iddat al-wafat) is not necessary according to the Malikis. Rather, the judge's verdict is only necessary at the time of the first application, when the judge heard the woman's claim and after investigation granted four years of waiting. Only that time requires the judge's verdict. But caution is that when those four years that the judge appointed are completed, one should reapply and obtain from the judge a ruling of the missing person's death, so that in our madhhab there is concession to the extent of ease. However, in a place where reapplying to the judge or presenting a case is very difficult, there is no harm in acting without presenting a case a second time.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal stratagem (hila) for the wife of a missing person (zawjah mafqud). The Hanafi position, as presented, requires a judge's verdict to dissolve the marriage after the missing person is presumed dead. Initially, the woman must wait four years from the judge's investigation. After this period, it is recommended to obtain a second verdict confirming death for precaution, but the Maliki school does not require it. Thanwi notes that if reapplying is difficult, the woman may act without the second verdict, making an exception for hardship.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A missing person whose whereabouts and life/death status are unknown.
زوجہ مفقود (zawjah mafqud): The wife of a missing person.
عدت وفات (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period after the death of a husband (four months and ten days).
مشقت (mashaqqah): Hardship or difficulty that allows relaxation of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She must follow the Maliki school.

Key Takeaway
The second verdict is a recommended precaution, not an obligation; hardship permits its omission.

Hanafi Ruling for Wife of Missing Person in Dar al-Harb
Explains Hanafi vs. Ashhab ruling for dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the wife of a missing person in dar al-harb, compare it with the Maliki (Ashhab) position, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a real scenario.

Core Idea
In dar al-harb, the Hanafi school bars remarriage until natural death expectancy, while Ashhab allows dissolution after four years.

The status of a missing person's wife becomes even more complex when the husband disappears in non-Muslim territory (dar al-harb). The Hanafi school applies a stricter ruling based on the difficulty of obtaining reliable news, while a Maliki scholar, Ashhab, argues for parity with dar al-islam. This lesson unpacks that difference and its practical consequences for the wife and the judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Context: Dar al-Harb vs Dar al-Islam
First, recall that dar al-harb is a land where Islamic law is not enforced. The Hanafi school treats missing persons in dar al-harb differently due to the unpredictability of communication and the presumption that the husband is still alive (istishab).
The passage begins by reminding us that the ruling for dar al-Islam was already mentioned; now focus shifts to dar al-harb.
2
State the Hanafi Position
According to the Hanafi school (jumhur), the wife cannot remarry as long as peers of the missing husband are still alive. This means she must wait until the natural death expectancy of such peers, often decades.
The key term here is 'peers' (hum 'umr) – people of similar age and health.
3
Recognize the Maliki (Ashhab) Position
Ashhab, a prominent Maliki jurist, holds that the same rules as in dar al-Islam apply: after the judge's four-year waiting period and investigation, the marriage may be dissolved.
Ashhab's view is based on the wife's hardship and the general principle that the rules of dar al-Islam are the default.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: Under Hanafi, the wife may wait indefinitely (possibly until she becomes elderly). Under Ashhab, she can seek dissolution after four years of disappearance and judicial investigation.
The thamarah (outcome) directly affects the wife's ability to remarry and her financial situation.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the stricter view: no remarriage until peers die. This is because the truth in dar al-harb is uncertain, and caution (ihtiyat) is prioritized.
The Hanafi response in the dispute map highlights istishab and the difficulty of investigation.

For the wife of a missing person in dar al-harb and dar al-islam, the aforementioned ruling applied in dar al-islam. And in dar al-harb, the ruling of the wife of the missing person according to the majority (jumhur) is the same ruling as that of the Hanafis, i.e., as long as his peers are alive, there is no way for his wife to separate from his marriage and marry another. But according to Ashhab (who is a distinguished student of Imam Malik and high-ranking in Maliki fiqh), in dar al-harb the same ruling applies as that which passed in dar al-islam. The answer to the question: when will the period of four years waiting that the ruler appoints begin? It will start from the time when the ruler or the Shari'ah committee becomes hopeless of finding out after investigation and inquiry. Whatever time passed before reaching the judge's court and his investigation will not be counted. The answer to the third question: It is obligatory for the wife of a missing person to obtain a verdict from the judge. The wife of the missing person is not independent to exit from his marriage in any way, rather obtaining the judge's verdict is necessary in every situation. The fourth question: It is upon the judge that he should not rely only on the investigation and statement of the woman and her guardians, but he himself should have search conducted. And the method of search is that the judge and ruler should send people to places where there is a strong assumption of the missing person going. And in places where there is no strong assumption, if they consider a letter sufficient, then send a letter to investigate. If there is hope of news by publishing in newspapers, that should also be done. In short, he should exert full effort and diligence in investigation. And after search, when hopelessness of finding occurs, then according to the aforementioned method, he should order the waiting period of four years. And the expenses of the search: There is a difference of opinion. Some said it is upon the woman, some said it is upon the public treasury (bayt al-mal). According to some, there is a detail: if the wife has wealth, then the search expenses are upon her, and this detail is the best.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers multiple issues: First, the Hanafi ruling for the wife of a missing person in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory) is stricter: she cannot remarry as long as peers of the missing husband are alive, meaning she must wait until the natural death expectancy. Imam Ashhab of the Maliki school holds that the same ruling applies in dar al-harb as in dar al-islam (i.e., four-year waiting and then dissolution). Second, the waiting period starts only after the judge's investigation, not from the time of disappearance. Third, obtaining the judge's verdict is absolutely necessary—she cannot self-terminate the marriage. Fourth, the judge must actively search, using letters or newspapers if needed. Fifth, the expenses of the search are to be borne by the wife if she has wealth; otherwise from the public treasury—the detailed view being preferred.

Key Terms
دارالحرب (dar al-harb): A land under non-Muslim rule, where Islamic law is not enforced.
دارالاسلام (dar al-islam): A land under Muslim rule, where Islamic law is established.
اشہب (Ashhab): A prominent student of Imam Malik, often cited in Maliki fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) After the judge declares the husband dead.

D) After two years if she is in hardship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the wife of a missing person in dar al-harb to wait until the natural death of her husband's peers, while Ashhab allows dissolution after four years.

Judge's Duties in Missing Person Cases: Search, Waiting Period, and Verdict
Lays out procedural obligations for the qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the procedural steps a judge must take for a missing person case, including the commencement of the waiting period, the obligation of a judicial verdict, and the allocation of search expenses.

Core Idea
The judge must actively investigate, the waiting period starts only after hopelessness, and the wife can never self-dissolve the marriage without a verdict.

When a husband disappears, the judge plays a central role. This lesson details the judge's duties: conducting a thorough search, determining when the four-year waiting period begins, the absolute necessity of a judicial verdict for dissolution, and how to cover search expenses. These rules ensure that marriages are not dissolved hastily and that the wife's rights are protected.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Judge's Duty to Investigate
The passage emphasizes that the judge cannot rely solely on the woman or her family's statements. He must actively search by sending people, letters, or using newspapers where likely to yield news.
This duty is obligatory (wajib) on the judge.
2
Define the Starting Point of the Waiting Period
The four-year waiting period does not start from the disappearance, but only after the judge (or Shari'ah committee) has investigated and become hopeless of finding any trace.
Time before investigation is not counted.
3
Recognize the Necessity of the Judge's Verdict
The wife is never independent to exit the marriage on her own. In every case, she must obtain a verdict from the judge to dissolve the marriage.
This is a mandatory (lazim) step; without it, any remarriage would be invalid.
4
Learn the Method of Search
The judge should send investigators to places where the missing person is likely to have gone. In unlikely places, letters may suffice. If newspapers help, use them. The goal is full exertion (juhd tabligh).
The phrase 'جہد تبلیغ' indicates the maximum effort.
5
Allocate Search Expenses
There is a difference: some say the wife pays, some say the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The preferred detail is: if the wife has wealth, she pays; otherwise, bayt al-mal bears the cost.
The author declares this detail as the best (أحسن).

Source Text

اور دارالحرب میں مقصود کی بیوی کاحکم جمہور الیہ کے نزدیک نورہ ہی حکم ہے جو حنفیہ کے نزدیک ہے یعنی جب تک اس کے ہم عمر لوگ زندہ ہیں اس وقت تک اس کی بیوی کے لیے اس کے نکاح سے علیحدہ ہونے اور دوسرا نکاح کرنے کی کوئی صورت نہیں لیکن اشہب کے نزدیک (جو کہ حضرت امام مالک کے ممتاز شاگردوں میں سے ہیں) اور فقہادر مالکیہ ہی بلند درجہ رکھتے ہیں دارالحرب میں بھی زوجہ مفقود کا وہ ہی حکم ہے جو

اور تفتیش کے مصارف مقصود کی تفتیش کے اخراجات کس کے ذمہ ہیں۔ کیا بات تقرا کے دیکھیں
اختلاف ہے، بعض نے کہا کہ عورت کے ذمہ ہے اور بعینی نے کہا بیت المال کے زمادر بعض کے نزدیک تفصیل ہے کہ اگر زوجہ کے پاس مال ہو تو مصارف تفتیش اس کے
علہ اور یہ تفصیل سب سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔
For the wife of a missing person in dar al-harb and dar al-islam, the aforementioned ruling applied in dar al-islam. And in dar al-harb, the ruling of the wife of the missing person according to the majority (jumhur) is the same ruling as that of the Hanafis, i.e., as long as his peers are alive, there is no way for his wife to separate from his marriage and marry another. But according to Ashhab (who is a distinguished student of Imam Malik and high-ranking in Maliki fiqh), in dar al-harb the same ruling applies as that which passed in dar al-islam. The answer to the question: when will the period of four years waiting that the ruler appoints begin? It will start from the time when the ruler or the Shari'ah committee becomes hopeless of finding out after investigation and inquiry. Whatever time passed before reaching the judge's court and his investigation will not be counted. The answer to the third question: It is obligatory for the wife of a missing person to obtain a verdict from the judge. The wife of the missing person is not independent to exit from his marriage in any way, rather obtaining the judge's verdict is necessary in every situation. The fourth question: It is upon the judge that he should not rely only on the investigation and statement of the woman and her guardians, but he himself should have search conducted. And the method of search is that the judge and ruler should send people to places where there is a strong assumption of the missing person going. And in places where there is no strong assumption, if they consider a letter sufficient, then send a letter to investigate. If there is hope of news by publishing in newspapers, that should also be done. In short, he should exert full effort and diligence in investigation. And after search, when hopelessness of finding occurs, then according to the aforementioned method, he should order the waiting period of four years. And the expenses of the search: There is a difference of opinion. Some said it is upon the woman, some said it is upon the public treasury (bayt al-mal). According to some, there is a detail: if the wife has wealth, then the search expenses are upon her, and this detail is the best.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers multiple issues: First, the Hanafi ruling for the wife of a missing person in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory) is stricter: she cannot remarry as long as peers of the missing husband are alive, meaning she must wait until the natural death expectancy. Imam Ashhab of the Maliki school holds that the same ruling applies in dar al-harb as in dar al-islam (i.e., four-year waiting and then dissolution). Second, the waiting period starts only after the judge's investigation, not from the time of disappearance. Third, obtaining the judge's verdict is absolutely necessary—she cannot self-terminate the marriage. Fourth, the judge must actively search, using letters or newspapers if needed. Fifth, the expenses of the search are to be borne by the wife if she has wealth; otherwise from the public treasury—the detailed view being preferred.

Key Terms
قاضی (qadi): A judge who issues binding verdicts in Islamic law.
جہد تبلیغ (juhd tabligh): Maximum effort in investigation, exerting all possible means.
بیت المال (bayt al-mal): Public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, search expenses are best allocated:
A) Always on the wife.
B) Always on the public treasury.
C) On the wife if she has wealth, otherwise on the public treasury.
D) On the husband's family.

Key Takeaway
The judge must independently investigate, the waiting period starts after hopelessness, a judicial verdict is obligatory for dissolution, and search expenses follow a flexible rule based on the wife's financial capacity.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems
intermediate

The Legal Stratagem (Hila) for the Missing Person's Wife in Non-Muslim Lands
Legal stratagem (hila) for missing person's wife in non-Muslim lands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa for the wife of a missing person in a non-Muslim land by applying the Hanafi stratagem of adopting the Maliki procedure.

Core Idea
In the absence of a shariah judge, the Hanafi mufti may advise following the Maliki school to dissolve the marriage of a missing person's wife.

The Hanafi school's strict requirement for the wife of a missing person to wait until the husband would reach 90 years of age often leads to hardship, especially in non-Muslim lands where no shariah court exists. Classical Hanafi scholars, including Ashraf Ali Thanwi, permitted a legal stratagem (hila) by adopting the Maliki procedure after a thorough investigation. This lesson focuses on the conditions for this stratagem, including the role of Muslim officials or a pious panel (panchayat) and the Maliki waiting period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The wife of a missing person is stuck in her marriage indefinitely under the default Hanafi ruling.
The default Hanafi position requires waiting until the missing person would turn 90 or evidence of death.
2
Recognize the hila
In the absence of a qadi, the Hanafi mufti may follow the Maliki school as a stratagem to dissolve the marriage.
This is a permissible use of cross-madhhab borrowing under necessity.
3
Determine the substitute authority
If a Muslim government official rules according to shariah, his ruling suffices. Otherwise, a panel of pious Muslims (panchayat) must investigate.
The panel must meet the conditions of justness and follow Maliki procedure.
4
Apply the Maliki waiting period
After thorough investigation and hopelessness, the wife observes four months and ten days (the iddah of a widow/divorcée), then she may remarry.
This replaces the Hanafi default of waiting until 90.
5
Ensure the ruling is formal
The wife cannot act alone; she must obtain a formal ruling from the panel or official.
The ruling is equivalent to a qadi's decree.

حکم دارالحرب اور دارالاسلام میں مختلف

It will only begin from the time when the ruler or the shariah committee becomes hopeless from finding the missing person after investigation and inquiry, and before reaching the qadi's court and his investigation, no matter how much time has passed, it will not be considered. The answer to the third question: It is necessary for the wife of the missing person to obtain a ruling from the qadi. That is, the wife of the missing person is not independent to leave the marriage in any way, rather in every case it is necessary to obtain the qadi's ruling. The answer to the fourth question: It is incumbent upon the qadi to investigate the missing person. The qadi should not be content with merely questioning the woman and her guardians, but he should also conduct his own search. The method of search is that the qadi and the ruler should send people to wherever there is a strong presumption that the missing person went, and where there is no strong presumption, if they consider a letter sufficient, they should send a letter to investigate, and if there is hope of getting news by publishing in newspapers, he should do that too. In short, he should exert full effort and diligence in the investigation. And when after searching he becomes hopeless of finding him, then at that time he should order a waiting period of four months and ten days according to the method mentioned earlier. And the costs of the investigation: There is a difference of opinion. Some said it is upon the woman, and some said it is upon the treasury. According to some, there is a detail: if the wife has wealth, the costs are upon her; this detail is the best. They will be upon her, otherwise upon the treasury, and where there is no treasury, like India etc., if the government bears the costs, it is better, otherwise a collection from the Muslims may be made. The answer to the fifth question: In cities where there is no shariah judge, like the condition of all cities in India except Islamic states, the Muslim officials who have authority from the government to settle such matters, if they are Muslims and decide according to shariah, their decision also becomes a substitute for the qadi's ruling, as has been detailed in the introduction to the second part. And if a Muslim official is not present or his court does not decide according to shariah, then according to the Maliki school, a group of religious Muslims should form a panel (panchayat) and investigate as described, and after complete investigation, if they give a shariah ruling, that ruling will also be considered in the place of the qadi's ruling. But it is necessary that the panel complies with the conditions that have been mentioned earlier; they should be carefully looked at. If the wife of the missing person goes to a place where a shariah judge or a Muslim ruler is present, and she files a case with him, his ruling is sufficient for the wife of the missing person. However, if the wife of an insane man or the wife of an impotent man goes alone to the jurisdiction of a qadi, the qadi's ruling will be valid. Rather, it is necessary that the insane man and the impotent man are also in the same jurisdiction. The answer to the sixth question: The ruling of a missing person differs in dar al-harb and dar al-Islam, as has been detailed in the answer to the first question. But in the fatwas of Maliki scholars...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive legal ruling (hila) from the Hanafi tradition, specifically addressing the problem of the missing person's (mafqud) wife. The text emphasizes that the wife's waiting period (iddah) does not begin merely with the passage of time; rather, it starts only after the qadi (or a shariah committee) has conducted a thorough investigation and become hopeless of finding the missing person. The wife cannot independently dissolve the marriage; she must obtain a formal ruling from the qadi. The qadi is duty-bound to conduct an active search, using all available means—sending agents, correspondence, and even newspaper advertisements—until he is convinced that further effort is futile. Only then does he order the wife to observe a waiting period of four months and ten days (the iddah of a widow or divorced woman, though here applied as the minimum before she can remarry according to the Maliki school adopted as a stratagem). The costs of investigation are a point of scholarly difference: the preferred view is that if the wife has wealth, she bears the costs; otherwise, they are borne by the public treasury (bayt al-mal). In the absence of a treasury (as in non-Muslim states like India), the expense should ideally be covered by the government, and if not, by a collection from the Muslim community. In lands without a shariah judge, the Hanafi author permits recourse to the Maliki school: if a Muslim government official rules according to shariah, his ruling suffices; otherwise, a panel of pious Muslims (panchayat) may investigate and issue a ruling, provided they meet the conditions outlined earlier. A key procedural point: if the wife goes to a jurisdiction where a shariah judge exists, his ruling is valid even if the husband is in another jurisdiction. However, for the wife of an insane (majnun) or impotent ('innin) husband, it is necessary that the husband also be in the same jurisdiction for the qadi's ruling to be effective. The passage concludes by noting that the ruling for a missing person differs between dar al-harb (abode of war) and dar al-Islam, a topic detailed in the answer to the first question, and references Maliki fatwas.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life or death is uncertain.
قاضي شرعي (qadi shar'i): A judge appointed by a legitimate Muslim authority to decide legal matters according to shariah.
تفتیش (taftish): Thorough investigation and search for the missing person, including inquiries, correspondence, and publication.
پنچایت (panchayat): A group of pious Muslims acting as a panel to investigate and issue a shariah ruling in the absence of a qadi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required before the missing person's wife can remarry in a non-Muslim land where no shariah judge exists?
A) She must wait until her husband reaches 90 years old.
B) She may remarry after four months of waiting without investigation.
C) A panel of pious Muslims must investigate, become hopeless, then issue a ruling and she observes four months and ten days.
D) She can simply declare herself divorced.

Key Takeaway
The wife of a missing person in a non-Muslim land may follow the Maliki procedure after a proper investigation by a piously panel, as a Hanafi legal stratagem.

The Qadi's Investigative Duty and the Trigger of the Waiting Period
Qadi's duty to investigate and start of waiting period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the qadi's duty to investigate the missing person and identify when the waiting period begins according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
The waiting period for the missing person's wife begins only after the qadi completes a thorough investigation and becomes hopeless.

Many assume that time alone dissolves the marriage, but the Hanafi school requires a formal process. The passage emphasizes that the waiting period does not start simply with the passage of time; it begins only after the qadi (or shariah committee) has exhausted all means of investigation and becomes hopeless. This lesson explores the qadi's duty, the methods of investigation, and the four-month and ten-day waiting period ordered after hopelessness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
The waiting period starts only when the qadi becomes hopeless after investigation.
Time spent before the qadi's hopelessness is not counted.
2
Describe the qadi's duty
The qadi must actively search; he cannot rely solely on the woman's statements.
He must exert full effort (juhd) in investigation.
3
List investigation methods
Methods include sending agents to likely places, correspondence, and newspaper announcements.
The qadi should use all available means.
4
Define hopelessness
Hopelessness is reached when all reasonable efforts fail to find the missing person.
It is a factual determination by the qadi.
5
Determine the waiting period
After hopelessness, the qadi orders a waiting period of four months and ten days.
This period is based on Maliki procedure (as per the hila).

مفقود کی بیوی کسی صورت میں اس کے نکاح سے خارج ہونے ہیں خود مختار

نہیں بلکہ ہر حالت میں قاضی کا فیصلہ حاصل کرنا ضروری ہے۔
قاضی کے ذمہ مفقود کی تارش و جواب سوال چهارم، با قاضی پر ھی نوری
ہے کہ صرف عورت اور اس کے اولیاء
کی تفتیش اور ان کے بیان پر اکتفانہ کرے بلکہ خود بھی تلاش کرائے ۔
اور تلاش کرنے کی صورت یہ ہے کہ قاضی اور حاکم کو جہاں جہاں مفقود کے جانے کا غالب گمان ہو وہاں وہاں آدمی بھیجا جاوے اور میں جگہ جانے کا غالب گمان نہ ہو صرفت اعمال ہو وہاں اگر خط کو کافی سمجھتے تو وہاں خطورا بھیج کر تحقیق کرے، اور اگر اخبار میں شائع کر دینے سے خبر ملنے کی امید ہوتو یہ بھی کرے ، الفرض تفتیش میں پوری کوشش
اور جہد تبلیغ کرے۔ کما لا یخفی۔ مع
اور تب تلاش کے بعد پتہ ملنے سے مایوسی ہو جائے اس وقت مذكورة الصدر طریق
پر چار ماں کے مزارا انتظار کا حکم کرے۔
سال
اور تفتیش کے مصارف مقصود کی تفتیش کے اخراجات کس کے ذمہ ہیں۔ کیا بات تقرا کے دیکھیں
اختلاف ہے، بعض نے کہا کہ عورت کے ذمہ ہے اور بعینی نے کہا بیت المال کے زمادر بعض کے نزدیک تفصیل ہے کہ اگر زوجہ کے پاس مال ہو تو مصارف تفتیش اس کے
علہ اور یہ تفصیل سب سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔

286
ذمہ ہوں گے ورنہ بیت المال کے ذمہ راور جس جگہ بیت المال نہ ہو جیسے ہندوستان وغیرہ اگر ان مواقع میں حکومت وقت مصارف برداشت کرے تو بہتر ورنہ مسلمانوں سے
چندہ کر لیا جائے)
اجواب سوال پنجم، من بلاد میں مسلم حکام کے موافق شرعی فیصلہ کی حیثیت قاضی شرعی موجود نہیں جیسے
an
اسلامی ریاستوں کے علاوہ ہندوستان کے تمام شہروں کا حال ہے تو وہاں وہ حکام جو گیر نمنٹ کی طرف سے اس قسم کے معاملات کا تصفیہ کرنے کا اختیار رکھتے ہیں اگر وہ مسلمان ہوں اور فیصلہ شریعیت کے موافق کریں تو ان کا فیصلہ بھی فضائے قاضی کے قائم مقام ہو جاتا ہے جیسا کہ اس جزو دوم کے مقدمہ میں مفصل گذر چکا ہے اور اگر سلمان اکم موجود نہ ہو یا اس کی عدالت سے فیصلہ شریعت کے مطابق نہ ہو تا ہو توپھر مذہب مالکیہ کے موافق دیندار مسلمانوں کی ایک جماعت پنجایت کرکے حسب بیان مذکور تحقیق کرے اور تحقیق کامل کے بعد شرعی فیصلہ صا کر دے تو یہ فیصلہ بھی قضائے قاضی کے حکم میں ہو جاوے گا، لیکن پنچایت کا ان شرائط کے موافق ہونا ضروری ہے جو مقد میں گذر چکی ہیں وہاں غور سے دیکھ لیا جائے۔ اگر زوجہ مفقود ایسی جگہ چلی جاوے جہاں قاضی شرعی با سلمان حاکم موجود ہوا در اس کے پاس مقدمہ دائر کر نے تو اس کا فیصلہ بھی زوجہ مفقود کے لیے کافی ہے ، لیکن مجنون کی بیوی یا نین کی بیوی تنہا کسی قاضی کے علاقہ میں چلی جائے تو قاضی کا فیصلہ معتبر ہو گا۔ بلکہ یہ ضروری ہے کہ مجنون وعنین بھی اسی قاضی کے علاقہ میں ہوں ۔
دارالحرب و دار السلام میں مفقود کا حکم سوال شے کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مفقود کا
BA
حکم دارالحرب اور دارالاسلام میں مختلف
ہے جیسا کہ سوال اول کے جواب میں تفصیلی طور پر گزر چکا لیکن علماء مالکیہ کے فتاوی
It will only begin from the time when the ruler or the shariah committee becomes hopeless from finding the missing person after investigation and inquiry, and before reaching the qadi's court and his investigation, no matter how much time has passed, it will not be considered. The answer to the third question: It is necessary for the wife of the missing person to obtain a ruling from the qadi. That is, the wife of the missing person is not independent to leave the marriage in any way, rather in every case it is necessary to obtain the qadi's ruling. The answer to the fourth question: It is incumbent upon the qadi to investigate the missing person. The qadi should not be content with merely questioning the woman and her guardians, but he should also conduct his own search. The method of search is that the qadi and the ruler should send people to wherever there is a strong presumption that the missing person went, and where there is no strong presumption, if they consider a letter sufficient, they should send a letter to investigate, and if there is hope of getting news by publishing in newspapers, he should do that too. In short, he should exert full effort and diligence in the investigation. And when after searching he becomes hopeless of finding him, then at that time he should order a waiting period of four months and ten days according to the method mentioned earlier. And the costs of the investigation: There is a difference of opinion. Some said it is upon the woman, and some said it is upon the treasury. According to some, there is a detail: if the wife has wealth, the costs are upon her; this detail is the best. They will be upon her, otherwise upon the treasury, and where there is no treasury, like India etc., if the government bears the costs, it is better, otherwise a collection from the Muslims may be made. The answer to the fifth question: In cities where there is no shariah judge, like the condition of all cities in India except Islamic states, the Muslim officials who have authority from the government to settle such matters, if they are Muslims and decide according to shariah, their decision also becomes a substitute for the qadi's ruling, as has been detailed in the introduction to the second part. And if a Muslim official is not present or his court does not decide according to shariah, then according to the Maliki school, a group of religious Muslims should form a panel (panchayat) and investigate as described, and after complete investigation, if they give a shariah ruling, that ruling will also be considered in the place of the qadi's ruling. But it is necessary that the panel complies with the conditions that have been mentioned earlier; they should be carefully looked at. If the wife of the missing person goes to a place where a shariah judge or a Muslim ruler is present, and she files a case with him, his ruling is sufficient for the wife of the missing person. However, if the wife of an insane man or the wife of an impotent man goes alone to the jurisdiction of a qadi, the qadi's ruling will be valid. Rather, it is necessary that the insane man and the impotent man are also in the same jurisdiction. The answer to the sixth question: The ruling of a missing person differs in dar al-harb and dar al-Islam, as has been detailed in the answer to the first question. But in the fatwas of Maliki scholars...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive legal ruling (hila) from the Hanafi tradition, specifically addressing the problem of the missing person's (mafqud) wife. The text emphasizes that the wife's waiting period (iddah) does not begin merely with the passage of time; rather, it starts only after the qadi (or a shariah committee) has conducted a thorough investigation and become hopeless of finding the missing person. The wife cannot independently dissolve the marriage; she must obtain a formal ruling from the qadi. The qadi is duty-bound to conduct an active search, using all available means—sending agents, correspondence, and even newspaper advertisements—until he is convinced that further effort is futile. Only then does he order the wife to observe a waiting period of four months and ten days (the iddah of a widow or divorced woman, though here applied as the minimum before she can remarry according to the Maliki school adopted as a stratagem). The costs of investigation are a point of scholarly difference: the preferred view is that if the wife has wealth, she bears the costs; otherwise, they are borne by the public treasury (bayt al-mal). In the absence of a treasury (as in non-Muslim states like India), the expense should ideally be covered by the government, and if not, by a collection from the Muslim community. In lands without a shariah judge, the Hanafi author permits recourse to the Maliki school: if a Muslim government official rules according to shariah, his ruling suffices; otherwise, a panel of pious Muslims (panchayat) may investigate and issue a ruling, provided they meet the conditions outlined earlier. A key procedural point: if the wife goes to a jurisdiction where a shariah judge exists, his ruling is valid even if the husband is in another jurisdiction. However, for the wife of an insane (majnun) or impotent ('innin) husband, it is necessary that the husband also be in the same jurisdiction for the qadi's ruling to be effective. The passage concludes by noting that the ruling for a missing person differs between dar al-harb (abode of war) and dar al-Islam, a topic detailed in the answer to the first question, and references Maliki fatwas.

Key Terms
تفتیش (taftish): Thorough investigation and search for the missing person, including inquiries, correspondence, and publication.
مفقود (mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life or death is uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary duty of the qadi regarding a missing person's wife before ordering the waiting period?
A) To accept the wife's testimony and issue a divorce.
B) To conduct his own investigation using all available means.
C) To wait indefinitely.
D) To ask the wife to bring witnesses for the disappearance.

Key Takeaway
The waiting period for the missing person's wife begins only after the qadi completes a thorough investigation and declares hopelessness.

Hila Shariyyah — Legal Stratagems
intermediate
6 min
Financing the Investigation – Who Bears the Costs?
Costs of investigation debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different opinions on who bears the costs of investigating the missing person and apply the preferred Hanafi ruling in various scenarios.

Core Idea
The costs of investigating a missing person are borne by the wife if she is wealthy, otherwise by the treasury or the community.

A practical concern in missing person cases is who pays for the search. The passage presents a difference of opinion among scholars: some say the wife pays, some say the public treasury (bayt al-mal), and some detail that the wife pays if she has means, otherwise the treasury. Where no treasury exists, the government or a community collection covers the costs. This lesson examines these opinions and their modern applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the difference of opinion
Scholars differ on who bears the investigation costs.
Three views: wife, treasury, or detail based on wife's wealth.
2
State the preferred view
The passage says the detail (if wife has wealth, she pays; otherwise treasury) is the best.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Apply the ruling to scenarios
If wife has money, she pays; if not, the government or community pays.
In non-Muslim lands without treasury, the government should bear costs, or a collection from Muslims.

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive legal ruling (hila) from the Hanafi tradition, specifically addressing the problem of the missing person's (mafqud) wife. The text emphasizes that the wife's waiting period (iddah) does not begin merely with the passage of time; rather, it starts only after the qadi (or a shariah committee) has conducted a thorough investigation and become hopeless of finding the missing person. The wife cannot independently dissolve the marriage; she must obtain a formal ruling from the qadi. The qadi is duty-bound to conduct an active search, using all available means—sending agents, correspondence, and even newspaper advertisements—until he is convinced that further effort is futile. Only then does he order the wife to observe a waiting period of four months and ten days (the iddah of a widow or divorced woman, though here applied as the minimum before she can remarry according to the Maliki school adopted as a stratagem). The costs of investigation are a point of scholarly difference: the preferred view is that if the wife has wealth, she bears the costs; otherwise, they are borne by the public treasury (bayt al-mal). In the absence of a treasury (as in non-Muslim states like India), the expense should ideally be covered by the government, and if not, by a collection from the Muslim community. In lands without a shariah judge, the Hanafi author permits recourse to the Maliki school: if a Muslim government official rules according to shariah, his ruling suffices; otherwise, a panel of pious Muslims (panchayat) may investigate and issue a ruling, provided they meet the conditions outlined earlier. A key procedural point: if the wife goes to a jurisdiction where a shariah judge exists, his ruling is valid even if the husband is in another jurisdiction. However, for the wife of an insane (majnun) or impotent ('innin) husband, it is necessary that the husband also be in the same jurisdiction for the qadi's ruling to be effective. The passage concludes by noting that the ruling for a missing person differs between dar al-harb (abode of war) and dar al-Islam, a topic detailed in the answer to the first question, and references Maliki fatwas.

Key Terms
بیت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state, used for public welfare.
چندہ (chandah): Collection (voluntary contributions) from the Muslim community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best view regarding the costs of investigating the missing person?
A) The wife always bears the costs.
B) The public treasury always bears the costs.
C) The wife bears the costs if she has wealth, otherwise the treasury or community bears them.
D) The government always bears the costs.

Key Takeaway
The costs of investigating a missing person are borne by the wife if she is wealthy, otherwise by the public treasury or community.

Jurisdictional Nuances in Missing Person Cases
Jurisdictional rules for wife of missing vs insane/impotent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the jurisdictional rules for the wife of a missing person compared to the wife of an insane (majnun) or impotent ('innin) husband, and apply them.

Core Idea
The wife of an insane or impotent husband must file in the same jurisdiction as her husband, while the missing person's wife may file wherever she goes.

The passage includes a nuanced jurisdictional rule: a missing person's wife can travel to a qadi anywhere and obtain a ruling, but if the wife is seeking dissolution due to her husband's insanity or impotence, she must file in the same jurisdiction as her husband. This distinction reflects different legal considerations: for the missing person, the focus is on the wife's interest, while for the insane/impotent, the husband's condition requires local adjudication. This lesson examines this procedural distinction and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between the wife of a missing person and the wife of an insane/impotent man.
Both involve marriage dissolution but with different jurisdictional rules.
2
State the rule for the missing person's wife
She can go to any qadi or Muslim ruler and obtain a valid ruling.
Her ruling is sufficient even if the husband is elsewhere.
3
State the rule for the insane/impotent wife
She must file in the qadi's jurisdiction where the husband resides; the qadi's ruling is only valid if the husband is in that jurisdiction.
The husband's presence in the jurisdiction is necessary.
4
Explain the rationale
For the missing person, the qadi's ruling is based on investigation and the wife's situation; for the insane/impotent, the husband's condition must be verified locally.
The rationale is that the defect (insanity/impotence) is attached to the husband's person and place.

حکم دارالحرب اور دارالاسلام میں مختلف
ہے جیسا کہ سوال اول کے جواب میں تفصیلی طور پر گزر چکا لیکن علماء مالکیہ کے فتاوی
It will only begin from the time when the ruler or the shariah committee becomes hopeless from finding the missing person after investigation and inquiry, and before reaching the qadi's court and his investigation, no matter how much time has passed, it will not be considered. The answer to the third question: It is necessary for the wife of the missing person to obtain a ruling from the qadi. That is, the wife of the missing person is not independent to leave the marriage in any way, rather in every case it is necessary to obtain the qadi's ruling. The answer to the fourth question: It is incumbent upon the qadi to investigate the missing person. The qadi should not be content with merely questioning the woman and her guardians, but he should also conduct his own search. The method of search is that the qadi and the ruler should send people to wherever there is a strong presumption that the missing person went, and where there is no strong presumption, if they consider a letter sufficient, they should send a letter to investigate, and if there is hope of getting news by publishing in newspapers, he should do that too. In short, he should exert full effort and diligence in the investigation. And when after searching he becomes hopeless of finding him, then at that time he should order a waiting period of four months and ten days according to the method mentioned earlier. And the costs of the investigation: There is a difference of opinion. Some said it is upon the woman, and some said it is upon the treasury. According to some, there is a detail: if the wife has wealth, the costs are upon her; this detail is the best. They will be upon her, otherwise upon the treasury, and where there is no treasury, like India etc., if the government bears the costs, it is better, otherwise a collection from the Muslims may be made. The answer to the fifth question: In cities where there is no shariah judge, like the condition of all cities in India except Islamic states, the Muslim officials who have authority from the government to settle such matters, if they are Muslims and decide according to shariah, their decision also becomes a substitute for the qadi's ruling, as has been detailed in the introduction to the second part. And if a Muslim official is not present or his court does not decide according to shariah, then according to the Maliki school, a group of religious Muslims should form a panel (panchayat) and investigate as described, and after complete investigation, if they give a shariah ruling, that ruling will also be considered in the place of the qadi's ruling. But it is necessary that the panel complies with the conditions that have been mentioned earlier; they should be carefully looked at. If the wife of the missing person goes to a place where a shariah judge or a Muslim ruler is present, and she files a case with him, his ruling is sufficient for the wife of the missing person. However, if the wife of an insane man or the wife of an impotent man goes alone to the jurisdiction of a qadi, the qadi's ruling will be valid. Rather, it is necessary that the insane man and the impotent man are also in the same jurisdiction. The answer to the sixth question: The ruling of a missing person differs in dar al-harb and dar al-Islam, as has been detailed in the answer to the first question. But in the fatwas of Maliki scholars...

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive legal ruling (hila) from the Hanafi tradition, specifically addressing the problem of the missing person's (mafqud) wife. The text emphasizes that the wife's waiting period (iddah) does not begin merely with the passage of time; rather, it starts only after the qadi (or a shariah committee) has conducted a thorough investigation and become hopeless of finding the missing person. The wife cannot independently dissolve the marriage; she must obtain a formal ruling from the qadi. The qadi is duty-bound to conduct an active search, using all available means—sending agents, correspondence, and even newspaper advertisements—until he is convinced that further effort is futile. Only then does he order the wife to observe a waiting period of four months and ten days (the iddah of a widow or divorced woman, though here applied as the minimum before she can remarry according to the Maliki school adopted as a stratagem). The costs of investigation are a point of scholarly difference: the preferred view is that if the wife has wealth, she bears the costs; otherwise, they are borne by the public treasury (bayt al-mal). In the absence of a treasury (as in non-Muslim states like India), the expense should ideally be covered by the government, and if not, by a collection from the Muslim community. In lands without a shariah judge, the Hanafi author permits recourse to the Maliki school: if a Muslim government official rules according to shariah, his ruling suffices; otherwise, a panel of pious Muslims (panchayat) may investigate and issue a ruling, provided they meet the conditions outlined earlier. A key procedural point: if the wife goes to a jurisdiction where a shariah judge exists, his ruling is valid even if the husband is in another jurisdiction. However, for the wife of an insane (majnun) or impotent ('innin) husband, it is necessary that the husband also be in the same jurisdiction for the qadi's ruling to be effective. The passage concludes by noting that the ruling for a missing person differs between dar al-harb (abode of war) and dar al-Islam, a topic detailed in the answer to the first question, and references Maliki fatwas.

Key Terms
مجنون (majnun): An insane person, legally incompetent in certain matters.
عنّين (‘innin): An impotent man, unable to consummate marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the jurisdictional condition for the wife of an insane man (majnun) to obtain a valid qadi's ruling?
A) She can file in any jurisdiction.
B) She must file in the jurisdiction where her husband resides.
C) She must file in the jurisdiction where the marriage took place.
D) She must file in the capital city.

Key Takeaway
The missing person's wife may obtain a ruling from any qadi, but the wife of an insane or impotent man must file in the husband's jurisdiction.
